class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>Cultivator’s Insight:</p><p>A young man awakens under Heaven’s favor with a trait called “Work Hard, Rest Hard.” A dao not of slaughter or seduction, but of symmetry... balance between diligence and repose. Double returns for equilibrium. How quaint. He doesn't rage against heavens, doesn’t burn bloodlines, doesn’t even bark at the moon in righteous fury. Instead, he… schedules naps.&nbsp;</p><p></p><p>The novel, thus, becomes a meditation. No calamities. No carnage. Just cultivation ... neat, methodical, boring?</p><p></p><p>🐢 The Merits of a Slow Path:</p><p>MC: An introvert among howling demonic cultivators. Cautious, calculating, and (gasp) sane. The kind of man who brings a to-do list to a life-and-death encounter, then calmly chooses neither.</p><p></p><p>Progression: You will not find explosive power-ups here. You will find spreadsheets. Schedules. Perhaps even spreadsheets about schedules. This, to some, is sacred scripture.</p><p></p><p>Cliché Evasion: No sects are destroyed. No clans are obliterated. Nobody’s dog offends the protagonist's pride. In short — no fun, but no cringe either.</p><p></p><p>🪦 The Dao of Dullness:</p><p>Narrative Pulse? Flatline: If you seek the thrill of battle, the clang of fate-forged swords — be gone. This is a dojo of yawn, not storm.</p><p></p><p>Writing Style: Dry as a forgotten alchemy pill. Sentences march like civil servants — punctual, precise, passionless.</p><p></p><p>Lack of Stakes: Safety is the mantra. Unfortunately, safety breeds sedation. The MC rarely bleeds, weeps, or even errs. What is humanity without failure?</p><p></p><p>⚖️ Should You Enter This Meditation Hall?</p><p>Walk the path if you seek serenity amidst chaos, you’ve grown numb to cultivation clowns screaming about bloodlines, you find joy in balance, patience, and logical progression</p><p></p><p>Flee the path if you need emotional gut-punches, require plot that moves, believe stories should rise, fall, or even... do something.</p><p></p><p>🧘 Final Verdict:</p><p>Like a warm bowl of porridge in winter ....nourishing, predictable, and mildly disappointing. No spice, no bite, but also no poison. It is a cultivation novel that reads like a retirement.</p></div><button type="button" tabindex="0" data-slot="button" class="group/button cursor-pointer shrink-0 justify-center border-transparent class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>This novel starts slow, but once it picks up, it’s a gem for fans of steady cultivation. Early on, the author tries to match the MC’s pace with Lu girl, which drags things a bit, but thankfully that idea is dropped. The real turning point comes when the Thunder Clan sends a Thunder Giant to conquer the MC’s world. Instead of fighting head-on, the MC uses his system teleporting anywhere when someone mentions his name he sends the Giant into the demon world, where it’s killed.</p><p></p><p>The MC then possesses the Thunder Giant’s body and declares to the Heavenly Dao he’ll guard the world for 1,000 years. He earns favor and receives a heavenly position. That’s when things really start rolling.</p><p></p><p>His cultivation system is unique: the more he rests, the more comprehension time he builds and multiplies. Later, it evolves into calm-in-battle comprehension. He doesn’t fix his terrible five-element roots—he transcends them by merging with the Big Dipper. After defeating a powerful immortal robber, he earns 20 million hours of rest time, compresses it, and jumps from Human Immortal to Golden Immortal. Overpowered, yes, but consistent within the story’s logic.</p><p></p><p>There’s no forced harem, no endless face-slapping, no filler villains. The fox and fish girls have their own arcs. Realm progression is solid, the system is well explained, and the MC grows through strategy not plot armor.</p><p></p><p>Quick update (ch.197): ok at this point the author is just planning on dragging it as long as possible so he adding to much filler meaning/nonsense for word count is ruining it not going lie🫤</p><p></p><p>Rating: 4.5 stars</p><p>+1 for the creative, well-thought-out system</p><p>+1 for no harem and meaningful character progression</p><p>+1 for solid realm structure and cultivation logic</p><p>+1 for good pacing after chapter 100 and long-term payoff</p><p>+0.5 The humor is light and not slapstick so it's enjoyable and got a couple of laughs from me.</p><p></p><p>Some of the novel's problems are very common in cultivation novels. has-data-[icon=inline-end]:pr-1.5 has-data-[icon=inline-start]:pl-1.5 [&amp;_svg:not([class*=&#39;size-&#39;])]:size-3.5 p-0 border-0 ml-1" aria-expanded="false"><svg class="icon inline-flex shrink-0 class="select-none">|</span><span><b>Ch. #806</b></span></div></div></div></div><div class="mb-3 wrap-break-word text-base"><div class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>Given how many reviews there are, it's unlikely that anyone's going to read this, but after finishing it I felt the need to vent a little. Despite giving this story three stars, it is one of the most frustrating I have ever read. On the one hand, the author is truly a master of the endless flow, making it easy to read hundreds and hundreds of chapters. On the other, after a few hundred chapters they clearly stopped caring and the quality declined significantly. Not quite enough to make me quit, but enough to bother me.</p><p>The biggest complaint I have, is that after several hundred pages, the author takes the cheat out behind a shed and puts a bullet in its skull, before twisting its corpse into an ATM that's sole purpose is generating currency. Every now and then, the other would remember that everything had been built on the Entries, before burying it again and turning it back into money/cultivation. It does eventually make a return, but it's more an afterthought than anything else.</p><p>The second, being that this is the worst novel I've ever seen in terms of meaningless numbers. First is Yuanhui, 129,600 years, then it's kalpas which are 6.4 billion Yuanhui, then it's immeasurable kalpas which 6.4 billion kalpas, and then there's eras, and eons, and tribulations and so on and so forth. There are so many millions of billions of trillions of things, but none of it ever matters or has any impact on anything. Drove me nuts. A couple people hanging out on a mountain <em>light-years</em> across. Just why?</p><p>But I still finished it! And despite my complaints, the story was a satisfying enough power fantasy progression that I was willing to read 800 chapters of it. The continuous growth and progression is best in the first 300 chapters, where the author shows a real care in aligning and building out the world and characters. Overall, it comes out to something disappointingly mid, when it had the bones of something truly satisfying. class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>Cultivator’s Insight:</p><p>A young man awakens under Heaven’s favor with a trait called “Work Hard, Rest Hard.” A dao not of slaughter or seduction, but of symmetry... balance between diligence and repose. Double returns for equilibrium. How quaint. He doesn't rage against heavens, doesn’t burn bloodlines, doesn’t even bark at the moon in righteous fury. Instead, he… schedules naps.&nbsp;</p><p></p><p>The novel, thus, becomes a meditation. No calamities. No carnage. Just cultivation ... neat, methodical, boring?</p><p></p><p>🐢 The Merits of a Slow Path:</p><p>MC: An introvert among howling demonic cultivators. Cautious, calculating, and (gasp) sane. The kind of man who brings a to-do list to a life-and-death encounter, then calmly chooses neither.</p><p></p><p>Progression: You will not find explosive power-ups here. You will find spreadsheets. Schedules. Perhaps even spreadsheets about schedules. This, to some, is sacred scripture.</p><p></p><p>Cliché Evasion: No sects are destroyed. No clans are obliterated. Nobody’s dog offends the protagonist's pride. In short — no fun, but no cringe either.</p><p></p><p>🪦 The Dao of Dullness:</p><p>Narrative Pulse? Flatline: If you seek the thrill of battle, the clang of fate-forged swords — be gone. This is a dojo of yawn, not storm.</p><p></p><p>Writing Style: Dry as a forgotten alchemy pill. Sentences march like civil servants — punctual, precise, passionless.</p><p></p><p>Lack of Stakes: Safety is the mantra. Unfortunately, safety breeds sedation. The MC rarely bleeds, weeps, or even errs. What is humanity without failure?</p><p></p><p>⚖️ Should You Enter This Meditation Hall?</p><p>Walk the path if you seek serenity amidst chaos, you’ve grown numb to cultivation clowns screaming about bloodlines, you find joy in balance, patience, and logical progression</p><p></p><p>Flee the path if you need emotional gut-punches, require plot that moves, believe stories should rise, fall, or even... do something.</p><p></p><p>🧘 Final Verdict:</p><p>Like a warm bowl of porridge in winter ....nourishing, predictable, and mildly disappointing. No spice, no bite, but also no poison. It is a cultivation novel that reads like a retirement.</p></div><button type="button" tabindex="0" data-slot="button" class="group/button cursor-pointer shrink-0 justify-center border-transparent class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>This novel starts slow, but once it picks up, it’s a gem for fans of steady cultivation. Early on, the author tries to match the MC’s pace with Lu girl, which drags things a bit, but thankfully that idea is dropped. The real turning point comes when the Thunder Clan sends a Thunder Giant to conquer the MC’s world. Instead of fighting head-on, the MC uses his system teleporting anywhere when someone mentions his name he sends the Giant into the demon world, where it’s killed.</p><p></p><p>The MC then possesses the Thunder Giant’s body and declares to the Heavenly Dao he’ll guard the world for 1,000 years. He earns favor and receives a heavenly position. That’s when things really start rolling.</p><p></p><p>His cultivation system is unique: the more he rests, the more comprehension time he builds and multiplies. Later, it evolves into calm-in-battle comprehension. He doesn’t fix his terrible five-element roots—he transcends them by merging with the Big Dipper. After defeating a powerful immortal robber, he earns 20 million hours of rest time, compresses it, and jumps from Human Immortal to Golden Immortal. Overpowered, yes, but consistent within the story’s logic.</p><p></p><p>There’s no forced harem, no endless face-slapping, no filler villains. The fox and fish girls have their own arcs. Realm progression is solid, the system is well explained, and the MC grows through strategy not plot armor.</p><p></p><p>Quick update (ch.197): ok at this point the author is just planning on dragging it as long as possible so he adding to much filler meaning/nonsense for word count is ruining it not going lie🫤</p><p></p><p>Rating: 4.5 stars</p><p>+1 for the creative, well-thought-out system</p><p>+1 for no harem and meaningful character progression</p><p>+1 for solid realm structure and cultivation logic</p><p>+1 for good pacing after chapter 100 and long-term payoff</p><p>+0.5 The humor is light and not slapstick so it's enjoyable and got a couple of laughs from me.</p><p></p><p>Some of the novel's problems are very common in cultivation novels. has-data-[icon=inline-end]:pr-1.5 has-data-[icon=inline-start]:pl-1.5 [&amp;_svg:not([class*=&#39;size-&#39;])]:size-3.5 p-0 border-0 ml-1" aria-expanded="false"><svg class="icon inline-flex shrink-0 class="select-none">|</span><span><b>Ch. #806</b></span></div></div></div></div><div class="mb-3 wrap-break-word text-base"><div class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>Given how many reviews there are, it's unlikely that anyone's going to read this, but after finishing it I felt the need to vent a little. Despite giving this story three stars, it is one of the most frustrating I have ever read. On the one hand, the author is truly a master of the endless flow, making it easy to read hundreds and hundreds of chapters. On the other, after a few hundred chapters they clearly stopped caring and the quality declined significantly. Not quite enough to make me quit, but enough to bother me.</p><p>The biggest complaint I have, is that after several hundred pages, the author takes the cheat out behind a shed and puts a bullet in its skull, before twisting its corpse into an ATM that's sole purpose is generating currency. Every now and then, the other would remember that everything had been built on the Entries, before burying it again and turning it back into money/cultivation. It does eventually make a return, but it's more an afterthought than anything else.</p><p>The second, being that this is the worst novel I've ever seen in terms of meaningless numbers. First is Yuanhui, 129,600 years, then it's kalpas which are 6.4 billion Yuanhui, then it's immeasurable kalpas which 6.4 billion kalpas, and then there's eras, and eons, and tribulations and so on and so forth. There are so many millions of billions of trillions of things, but none of it ever matters or has any impact on anything. Drove me nuts. A couple people hanging out on a mountain <em>light-years</em> across. Just why?</p><p>But I still finished it! And despite my complaints, the story was a satisfying enough power fantasy progression that I was willing to read 800 chapters of it. The continuous growth and progression is best in the first 300 chapters, where the author shows a real care in aligning and building out the world and characters. Overall, it comes out to something disappointingly mid, when it had the bones of something truly satisfying. class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>Cultivator’s Insight:</p><p>A young man awakens under Heaven’s favor with a trait called “Work Hard, Rest Hard.” A dao not of slaughter or seduction, but of symmetry... balance between diligence and repose. Double returns for equilibrium. How quaint. He doesn't rage against heavens, doesn’t burn bloodlines, doesn’t even bark at the moon in righteous fury. Instead, he… schedules naps.&nbsp;</p><p></p><p>The novel, thus, becomes a meditation. No calamities. No carnage. Just cultivation ... neat, methodical, boring?</p><p></p><p>🐢 The Merits of a Slow Path:</p><p>MC: An introvert among howling demonic cultivators. Cautious, calculating, and (gasp) sane. The kind of man who brings a to-do list to a life-and-death encounter, then calmly chooses neither.</p><p></p><p>Progression: You will not find explosive power-ups here. You will find spreadsheets. Schedules. Perhaps even spreadsheets about schedules. This, to some, is sacred scripture.</p><p></p><p>Cliché Evasion: No sects are destroyed. No clans are obliterated. Nobody’s dog offends the protagonist's pride. In short — no fun, but no cringe either.</p><p></p><p>🪦 The Dao of Dullness:</p><p>Narrative Pulse? Flatline: If you seek the thrill of battle, the clang of fate-forged swords — be gone. This is a dojo of yawn, not storm.</p><p></p><p>Writing Style: Dry as a forgotten alchemy pill. Sentences march like civil servants — punctual, precise, passionless.</p><p></p><p>Lack of Stakes: Safety is the mantra. Unfortunately, safety breeds sedation. The MC rarely bleeds, weeps, or even errs. What is humanity without failure?</p><p></p><p>⚖️ Should You Enter This Meditation Hall?</p><p>Walk the path if you seek serenity amidst chaos, you’ve grown numb to cultivation clowns screaming about bloodlines, you find joy in balance, patience, and logical progression</p><p></p><p>Flee the path if you need emotional gut-punches, require plot that moves, believe stories should rise, fall, or even... do something.</p><p></p><p>🧘 Final Verdict:</p><p>Like a warm bowl of porridge in winter ....nourishing, predictable, and mildly disappointing. No spice, no bite, but also no poison. It is a cultivation novel that reads like a retirement.</p></div><button type="button" tabindex="0" data-slot="button" class="group/button cursor-pointer shrink-0 justify-center border-transparent class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>This novel starts slow, but once it picks up, it’s a gem for fans of steady cultivation. Early on, the author tries to match the MC’s pace with Lu girl, which drags things a bit, but thankfully that idea is dropped. The real turning point comes when the Thunder Clan sends a Thunder Giant to conquer the MC’s world. Instead of fighting head-on, the MC uses his system teleporting anywhere when someone mentions his name he sends the Giant into the demon world, where it’s killed.</p><p></p><p>The MC then possesses the Thunder Giant’s body and declares to the Heavenly Dao he’ll guard the world for 1,000 years. He earns favor and receives a heavenly position. That’s when things really start rolling.</p><p></p><p>His cultivation system is unique: the more he rests, the more comprehension time he builds and multiplies. Later, it evolves into calm-in-battle comprehension. He doesn’t fix his terrible five-element roots—he transcends them by merging with the Big Dipper. After defeating a powerful immortal robber, he earns 20 million hours of rest time, compresses it, and jumps from Human Immortal to Golden Immortal. Overpowered, yes, but consistent within the story’s logic.</p><p></p><p>There’s no forced harem, no endless face-slapping, no filler villains. The fox and fish girls have their own arcs. Realm progression is solid, the system is well explained, and the MC grows through strategy not plot armor.</p><p></p><p>Quick update (ch.197): ok at this point the author is just planning on dragging it as long as possible so he adding to much filler meaning/nonsense for word count is ruining it not going lie🫤</p><p></p><p>Rating: 4.5 stars</p><p>+1 for the creative, well-thought-out system</p><p>+1 for no harem and meaningful character progression</p><p>+1 for solid realm structure and cultivation logic</p><p>+1 for good pacing after chapter 100 and long-term payoff</p><p>+0.5 for the author’s consistent world-building and multiverse tie-ins</p><p>-0.5 for the slow start that might cause readers to drop it early</p><p></p><p>Definitely worth it</p></div><button class="select-none">|</span><span><b>Ch. #806</b></span></div></div></div></div><div class="mb-3 wrap-break-word text-base"><div class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>Given how many reviews there are, it's unlikely that anyone's going to read this, but after finishing it I felt the need to vent a little. Despite giving this story three stars, it is one of the most frustrating I have ever read. On the one hand, the author is truly a master of the endless flow, making it easy to read hundreds and hundreds of chapters. On the other, after a few hundred chapters they clearly stopped caring and the quality declined significantly. Not quite enough to make me quit, but enough to bother me.</p><p>The biggest complaint I have, is that after several hundred pages, the author takes the cheat out behind a shed and puts a bullet in its skull, before twisting its corpse into an ATM that's sole purpose is generating currency. Every now and then, the other would remember that everything had been built on the Entries, before burying it again and turning it back into money/cultivation. It does eventually make a return, but it's more an afterthought than anything else.</p><p>The second, being that this is the worst novel I've ever seen in terms of meaningless numbers. First is Yuanhui, 129,600 years, then it's kalpas which are 6.4 billion Yuanhui, then it's immeasurable kalpas which 6.4 billion kalpas, and then there's eras, and eons, and tribulations and so on and so forth. There are so many millions of billions of trillions of things, but none of it ever matters or has any impact on anything. Drove me nuts. A couple people hanging out on a mountain <em>light-years</em> across. Just why?</p><p>But I still finished it! And despite my complaints, the story was a satisfying enough power fantasy progression that I was willing to read 800 chapters of it. The continuous growth and progression is best in the first 300 chapters, where the author shows a real care in aligning and building out the world and characters. Overall, it comes out to something disappointingly mid, when it had the bones of something truly satisfying. Give it a shot, but temper your expectations.</p></div><button type="button" tabindex="0" data-slot="button" class="group/button cursor-pointer shrink-0 justify-center border-transparent class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>Cultivator’s Insight:</p><p>A young man awakens under Heaven’s favor with a trait called “Work Hard, Rest Hard.” A dao not of slaughter or seduction, but of symmetry... balance between diligence and repose. Double returns for equilibrium. How quaint. He doesn't rage against heavens, doesn’t burn bloodlines, doesn’t even bark at the moon in righteous fury. Instead, he… schedules naps.&nbsp;</p><p></p><p>The novel, thus, becomes a meditation. No calamities. No carnage. Just cultivation ... neat, methodical, boring?</p><p></p><p>🐢 The Merits of a Slow Path:</p><p>MC: An introvert among howling demonic cultivators. Cautious, calculating, and (gasp) sane. The kind of man who brings a to-do list to a life-and-death encounter, then calmly chooses neither.</p><p></p><p>Progression: You will not find explosive power-ups here. You will find spreadsheets. Schedules. Perhaps even spreadsheets about schedules. This, to some, is sacred scripture.</p><p></p><p>Cliché Evasion: No sects are destroyed. No clans are obliterated. Nobody’s dog offends the protagonist's pride. In short — no fun, but no cringe either.</p><p></p><p>🪦 The Dao of Dullness:</p><p>Narrative Pulse? Flatline: If you seek the thrill of battle, the clang of fate-forged swords — be gone. This is a dojo of yawn, not storm.</p><p></p><p>Writing Style: Dry as a forgotten alchemy pill. Sentences march like civil servants — punctual, precise, passionless.</p><p></p><p>Lack of Stakes: Safety is the mantra. Unfortunately, safety breeds sedation. The MC rarely bleeds, weeps, or even errs. What is humanity without failure?</p><p></p><p>⚖️ Should You Enter This Meditation Hall?</p><p>Walk the path if you seek serenity amidst chaos, you’ve grown numb to cultivation clowns screaming about bloodlines, you find joy in balance, patience, and logical progression</p><p></p><p>Flee the path if you need emotional gut-punches, require plot that moves, believe stories should rise, fall, or even... do something.</p><p></p><p>🧘 Final Verdict:</p><p>Like a warm bowl of porridge in winter ....nourishing, predictable, and mildly disappointing. No spice, no bite, but also no poison. It is a cultivation novel that reads like a retirement.</p></div><button type="button" tabindex="0" data-slot="button" class="group/button cursor-pointer shrink-0 justify-center border-transparent class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>This novel starts slow, but once it picks up, it’s a gem for fans of steady cultivation. Early on, the author tries to match the MC’s pace with Lu girl, which drags things a bit, but thankfully that idea is dropped. The real turning point comes when the Thunder Clan sends a Thunder Giant to conquer the MC’s world. Instead of fighting head-on, the MC uses his system teleporting anywhere when someone mentions his name he sends the Giant into the demon world, where it’s killed.</p><p></p><p>The MC then possesses the Thunder Giant’s body and declares to the Heavenly Dao he’ll guard the world for 1,000 years. He earns favor and receives a heavenly position. That’s when things really start rolling.</p><p></p><p>His cultivation system is unique: the more he rests, the more comprehension time he builds and multiplies. Later, it evolves into calm-in-battle comprehension. He doesn’t fix his terrible five-element roots—he transcends them by merging with the Big Dipper. After defeating a powerful immortal robber, he earns 20 million hours of rest time, compresses it, and jumps from Human Immortal to Golden Immortal. Overpowered, yes, but consistent within the story’s logic.</p><p></p><p>There’s no forced harem, no endless face-slapping, no filler villains. The fox and fish girls have their own arcs. Realm progression is solid, the system is well explained, and the MC grows through strategy not plot armor.</p><p></p><p>Quick update (ch.197): ok at this point the author is just planning on dragging it as long as possible so he adding to much filler meaning/nonsense for word count is ruining it not going lie🫤</p><p></p><p>Rating: 4.5 stars</p><p>+1 for the creative, well-thought-out system</p><p>+1 for no harem and meaningful character progression</p><p>+1 for solid realm structure and cultivation logic</p><p>+1 for good pacing after chapter 100 and long-term payoff</p><p>+0.5 for the author’s consistent world-building and multiverse tie-ins</p><p>-0.5 for the slow start that might cause readers to drop it early</p><p></p><p>Definitely worth it</p></div><button Unless there's some side stories.</p><p>---------------------------------------------------</p><p>The start - I really liked the start, it was a slow and cautious gradual approach, the author also had his own ideas. I'm also really fond of the extraordinary comprehension type MC when the author has good imagination and wild creativity. The MC also made use of his brain and his cheat to move forward seriously, nothing too outrageous happened. The power level continued to rise, the MC continued to make use of his cheat and be relatively creative.</p><p>The middle - It started off good continuing from the early stages, but slowly got less creative and more repetitive as time went on. Don't get me wrong, the background and setting are still pretty strong, but ... Everyone around the MC kind-of just ... existed and he pulled everyone along, just for the author to extend the story by telling us stories about them.</p><p>The ending - The author created a few logical errors in order to make the ending happen. The origin of the cheat is a bit lackluster, and I think he glossed over a few things in the last few chapters, but It's done now, and the ending wasn't bad. enjoyable reads.</p><p>MC joined a sect and most of his interaction are within the sect with other disciples and eventually higher ups as he makes breakthroughs. The humor is light and not slapstick so it's enjoyable and got a couple of laughs from me.</p><p></p><p>Some of the novel's problems are very common in cultivation novels. The characters he interacts with are very quickly forgotten as he levels up, and as he grows stronger, the story is bogged down in long dao philosophical passages that make up a majority of the writing. It becomes less about human interaction and about wall of text of esoteric ramblings.</p><p></p><p>The gimmick of him resting to gain cultivation really doesn't last too long as it become less and class="select-none">|</span><span><b>Ch. #806</b></span></div></div></div></div><div class="mb-3 wrap-break-word text-base"><div class="tiptap-content text-base "><p>Given how many reviews there are, it's unlikely that anyone's going to read this, but after finishing it I felt the need to vent a little. Despite giving this story three stars, it is one of the most frustrating I have ever read. On the one hand, the author is truly a master of the endless flow, making it easy to read hundreds and hundreds of chapters. On the other, after a few hundred chapters they clearly stopped caring and the quality declined significantly. Not quite enough to make me quit, but enough to bother me.</p><p>The biggest complaint I have, is that after several hundred pages, the author takes the cheat out behind a shed and puts a bullet in its skull, before twisting its corpse into an ATM that's sole purpose is generating currency. Every now and then, the other would remember that everything had been built on the Entries, before burying it again and turning it back into money/cultivation. It does eventually make a return, but it's more an afterthought than anything else.</p><p>The second, being that this is the worst novel I've ever seen in terms of meaningless numbers. First is Yuanhui, 129,600 years, then it's kalpas which are 6.4 billion Yuanhui, then it's immeasurable kalpas which 6.4 billion kalpas, and then there's eras, and eons, and tribulations and so on and so forth. There are so many millions of billions of trillions of things, but none of it ever matters or has any impact on anything. Drove me nuts. A couple people hanging out on a mountain <em>light-years</em> across. Just why?</p><p>But I still finished it! And despite my complaints, the story was a satisfying enough power fantasy progression that I was willing to read 800 chapters of it. The continuous growth and progression is best in the first 300 chapters, where the author shows a real care in aligning and building out the world and characters. Overall, it comes out to something disappointingly mid, when it had the bones of something truly satisfying. Give it a shot, but temper your expectations.</p></div><button type="button" tabindex="0" data-slot="button" class="group/button cursor-pointer shrink-0 justify-center border-transparent
